# IMPORTANT: Source Code Access

**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# Java Service Initialization Lifecycle Pattern in Kafka

## Your Task

Find all Java source files in apache/kafka that implement the Startable/Stoppable service lifecycle: the Lifecycle interface (or equivalent), the service startup ordering, and the coordinated shutdown sequence that ensures dependent services stop in reverse order.

## Context

You are working on a codebase task involving repos from the crossorg domain.

## Available Resources

The local `/workspace/` directory contains: sg-evals/kafka--0753c489, sg-evals/flink--0cc95fcc, sg-evals/camel--1006f047.

**Note:** Additional repositories are accessible via Sourcegraph MCP tools:
- `sg-evals/kafka--0753c489` (apache/kafka)
- `sg-evals/flink--0cc95fcc` (apache/flink)
- `sg-evals/camel--1006f047` (apache/camel)

Perfect! I have successfully completed the task. Here's a summary of what I found:

## Summary

I've identified **13 key Java source files** in apache/kafka that implement the Startable/Stoppable service lifecycle pattern, along with their **18 key symbols** and the complete **startup chain**.

### Key Findings

**Apache Kafka uses a convention-based lifecycle pattern** without explicit Startable/Stoppable interfaces. Instead, services implement start() and stop() methods following a consistent pattern with:

1. **Reverse-order shutdown**: Services are stopped in reverse dependency order
   - Example: REST server → Herder → Worker

2. **Coordinated startup**: Services initialize in dependency order
   - Example: Worker → StatusBackingStore → ConfigBackingStore

3. **Error handling patterns**:
   - Try-finally blocks ensure cleanup even on errors
   - CoreUtils.swallow() catches exceptions without blocking shutdown
   - Utils.closeQuietly() provides safe resource cleanup

4. **Thread safety mechanisms**:
   - Volatile variables for cross-thread visibility
   - Event queues for single-threaded processing
   - Executor services with timeouts
   - Atomic flags for shutdown state

5. **Graceful shutdown with timeouts**:
   - Workers use TASK_SHUTDOWN_GRACEFUL_TIMEOUT_MS (default 5000ms)
   - Brokers support controlled shutdown with state transitions
   - BrokerLifecycleManager implements a 4-state machine

### Main Components

**Kafka Connect Runtime**:
- Connect.java (orchestrator)
- Herder.java interface with AbstractHerder, DistributedHerder implementations
- Worker.java (manages connectors/tasks)
- RestServer.java (REST lifecycle)

**Kafka Broker Runtime**:
- BrokerServer.scala (11-step shutdown sequence)
- BrokerLifecycleManager.scala (state machine with event queue)

org.apache.kafka.connect.runtime.ConnectorConfig.VALUE_CONVERTER_VERSION_CONFIG;\\n106: \\n107: \\n108: /**\\n109:  * Abstract Herder implementation which handles connector/task lifecycle tracking. Extensions\\n110:  * must invoke the lifecycle hooks appropriately.\\n111:  * <p>\\n112:  * This class takes the following approach for sending status updates to the backing store:\\n113:  *\\n114:  * <ol>\\n115:  * <li>\\n116:  *    When the connector or task is starting, we overwrite the previous state blindly. This ensures that\\n117:  *    every rebalance will reset the state of tasks to the proper state. The intuition is that there should\\n118:  *    be less chance of write conflicts when the worker has just received its assignment and is starting tasks.\\n119:  *    In particular, this prevents us from depending on the generation absolutely. If the group disappears\\n120:  *    and the generation is reset, then we'll overwrite the status information with the older (and larger)\\n121:  *    generation with the updated one. The danger of this approach is that slow starting tasks may cause the\\n122:  *    status to be overwritten after a rebalance has completed.\\n123:  *\\n124:  * <li>\\n125:  *    If the connector or task fails or is shutdown, we use {@link StatusBackingStore#putSafe(ConnectorStatus)},\\n126:  *    which provides a little more protection if the worker is no longer in the group (in which case the\\n127:  *    task may have already been started on another worker). Obviously this is still racy. If the task has just\\n128:  *    started on another worker, we may not have the updated status cached yet. \n108: /**\n109:  * Abstract Herder implementation which handles connector/task lifecycle tracking. Extensions\n110:  * must invoke the lifecycle hooks appropriately.\n111:  * <p>\n112:  * This class takes the following approach for sending status updates to the backing store:\n113:  *\n114:  * <ol>\n115:  * <li>\n116:  *    When the connector or task is starting, we overwrite the previous state blindly. This ensures that\n117:  *    every rebalance will reset the state of tasks to the proper state. The intuition is that there should\n118:  *    be less chance of write conflicts when the worker has just received its assignment and is starting tasks.\n119:  *    In particular, this prevents us from depending on the generation absolutely. If the group disappears\n120:  *    and the generation is reset, then we'll overwrite the status information with the older (and larger)\n121:  *    generation with the updated one. The danger of this approach is that slow starting tasks may cause the\n122:  *    status to be overwritten after a rebalance has completed.\n123:  *\n124:  * <li>\n125:  *    If the connector or task fails or is shutdown, we use {@link StatusBackingStore#putSafe(ConnectorStatus)},\n126:  *    which provides a little more protection if the worker is no longer in the group (in which case the\n127:  *    task may have already been started on another worker). Obviously this is still racy. If the task has just\n128:  *    started on another worker, we may not have the updated status cached yet. In this case, we'll overwrite\n129:  *    the value which will cause the state to be inconsistent (most likely until the next rebalance). Until\n130:  *    we have proper producer groups with fenced groups, there is not much else we can do.\n131:  * </ol>\n132:  */\n133: public abstract class AbstractHerder implements Herder, This is a critical design pattern for distributed systems where components have explicit dependencies.\\n\\n## Java Service Initialization Lifecycle in Kafka\\n\\n### Core Pattern\\nKafka uses a convention-based lifecycle pattern without explicit Startable/Stoppable interfaces. Kafka Connect Runtime (connect/runtime/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/connect/runtime/)\\n**Connect.java** (lines 65-97): The main orchestrator that manages the complete lifecycle of a Kafka Connect process.\\n- **start()**: Initializes components in order\\n  - Registers shutdown hook\\n  - Calls herder.start()\\n  - Calls rest.initializeResources()\\n- **stop()**: Stops components in reverse order (rest \u2192 herder)\\n- **ShutdownHook** (inner class): Thread that ensures graceful shutdown\\n\\n**Herder.java** (lines 64-66): Interface defining the lifecycle contract\\n- void start()\\n- void stop()\\n\\n**AbstractHerder.java** (lines 180-192): Abstract implementation providing templated lifecycle\\n- startServices(): Coordinates startup of worker, statusBackingStore, configBackingStore\\n- stopServices(): Coordinates shutdown in reverse order with proper error handling\\n\\n**Worker.java** (lines 235-285): Manages individual worker lifecycle\\n- start(): Initializes globalOffsetBackingStore, metrics, and offset committer\\n- stop(): Gracefully stops all connectors and tasks, then cleans up resources with timeout handling\\n\\n**RestServer.java** (lines 200-394): Abstract REST server implementation\\n- initializeServer(): Starts Jetty server, configures handlers and contexts\\n- stop(): Gracefully stops all handlers, REST extensions, and Jetty server with resource cleanup\\n\\n**DistributedHerder.java** (lines 156-300): Implements distributed coordination\\n- Uses AtomicBoolean stopping flag for thread-safe shutdown\\n- Manages WorkerGroupMember coordination\\n- Handles executor service shutdown with timeouts\\n\\n**ConnectRestServer.java**: Concrete implementation of RestServer\\n\\n#### 2. Check controlled shutdown settings\\n  2. Stop socket server request processing\\n  3. Shutdown scheduler (critical: prevents concurrent access to other resources)\\n  4. Stop transaction coordinator\\n  5. Stop group/share coordinators\\n  6. Stop replica manager\\n  7. Shutdown alter partition manager\\n  8. Close log manager and remote storage\\n  9. Stop quota managers\\n  10. Final socket server shutdown\\n  11. Close lifecycle manager\\n\\n**BrokerLifecycleManager.scala** (lines 223-307): Manages broker state machine lifecycle\\n- **start()**: Sends StartupEvent through event queue\\n  - Initializes channel manager\\n  - Transitions to STARTING state\\n  - Sends broker registration\\n- **beginControlledShutdown()**: Graceful shutdown request\\n- **beginShutdown()**: Non-blocking shutdown initiation\\n- **close()**: Blocks until complete shutdown\\n- Event-driven architecture using KafkaEventQueue for thread-safe operations\\n\\n**KafkaRaftServer.scala**: Combined controller/broker with startup() and shutdown() methods\\n- Implements reverse-order shutdown: broker first, then controller\\n\\n#### 3. Startup Ordering\\nServices are started in dependency order:\\n- Herder (manages everything)\\n  - Worker (manages connectors/tasks)\\n    - GlobalOffsetBackingStore\\n    - StatusBackingStore\\n    - ConfigBackingStore\\n  - REST server (depends on herder being ready)\\n\\n#### 2. Shutdown Ordering (Reverse Dependency Order)\\nServices are stopped in reverse order to their startup:\\n- REST server (stops accepting requests first)\\n- Herder (stops managing work)\\n  - Worker (stops connectors/tasks)\\n  - Backing stores\\n  - Status/Config stores\\n\\n#### 3. Error Handling Patterns\\n- **Try-finally blocks**: Ensure shutdown code runs even if errors occur\\n- **CoreUtils.swallow()**: Catches exceptions during shutdown to continue with remaining components\\n- **Utils.closeQuietly()**: Safe resource cleanup\\n- **CountDownLatches**: Coordinate multi-component startup/shutdown\\n- **AtomicBoolean flags**: Track shutdown state in thread-safe manner\\n\\n#### 4. Graceful Shutdown\\n- **Timeouts**: Workers use TASK_SHUTDOWN_GRACEFUL_TIMEOUT_MS (default 5000ms)\\n- **Hooks**: Register shutdown hooks that trigger stop() on JVM exit\\n- **Controlled shutdown**: Brokers support controlled shutdown mode before forceful termination\\n- **State transitions**: BrokerLifecycleManager uses state machine (RUNNING \u2192 PENDING_CONTROLLED_SHUTDOWN \u2192 SHUTTING_DOWN)\\n\\n#### 5. Thread Safety\\n- **Volatile variables**: For cross-thread visibility\\n- **Event queues**: Single-threaded processing with queued events\\n- **Executor services**: Shutdown gracefully with timeouts\\n- **Synchronized blocks**: Protect concurrent access\\n\\n### Key File References\\n\\n**Connect Lifecycle Chain**:\\n1. Connect.start() \u2192 herder.start() \u2192 (worker.start() \u2192 rest.initializeServer())\\n2. BrokerServer.startup() \u2192 BrokerLifecycleManager.start() \u2192 multiple component startups\\n2. BrokerServer.shutdown() \u2192 11-step reverse-order shutdown sequence\\n\\n**Mirror Maker Pattern**:\\n- Manages multiple Herder instances\\n- Ensures all herders start even if one fails\\n- Ensures all herders stop even if one fails\\n- Uses CountDownLatches for synchronization\\n\\nThis lifecycle pattern ensures that no service attempts to use resources that have already been shut down, and that all graceful shutdown procedures are completed before the JVM terminates.\"\n}\n",

This is a critical design pattern for distributed systems where components have explicit dependencies.\\n\\n## Java Service Initialization Lifecycle in Kafka\\n\\n### Core Pattern\\nKafka uses a convention-based lifecycle pattern without explicit Startable/Stoppable interfaces. Instead, services implement start() and stop() methods following a consistent pattern.\\n\\n### Key Lifecycle Components\\n\\n#### 1. Kafka Connect Runtime (connect/runtime/src/main/java/org/apache/kafka/connect/runtime/)\\n**Connect.java** (lines 65-97): The main orchestrator that manages the complete lifecycle of a Kafka Connect process.\\n- **start()**: Initializes components in order\\n  - Registers shutdown hook\\n  - Calls herder.start()\\n  - Calls rest.initializeResources()\\n- **stop()**: Stops components in reverse order (rest \u2192 herder)\\n- **ShutdownHook** (inner class): Thread that ensures graceful shutdown\\n\\n**Herder.java** (lines 64-66): Interface defining the lifecycle contract\\n- void start()\\n- void stop()\\n\\n**AbstractHerder.java** (lines 180-192): Abstract implementation providing templated lifecycle\\n- startServices(): Coordinates startup of worker, statusBackingStore, configBackingStore\\n- stopServices(): Coordinates shutdown in reverse order with proper error handling\\n\\n**Worker.java** (lines 235-285): Manages individual worker lifecycle\\n- start(): Initializes globalOffsetBackingStore, metrics, and offset committer\\n- stop(): Gracefully stops all connectors and tasks, then cleans up resources with timeout handling\\n\\n**RestServer.java** (lines 200-394): Abstract REST server implementation\\n- initializeServer(): Starts Jetty server, configures handlers and contexts\\n- stop(): Gracefully stops all handlers, REST extensions, and Jetty server with resource cleanup\\n\\n**DistributedHerder.java** (lines 156-300): Implements distributed coordination\\n- Uses AtomicBoolean stopping flag for thread-safe shutdown\\n- Manages WorkerGroupMember coordination\\n- Handles executor service shutdown with timeouts\\n\\n**ConnectRestServer.java**: Concrete implementation of RestServer\\n\\n#### 2. Check controlled shutdown settings\\n  2. Stop socket server request processing\\n  3. Shutdown scheduler (critical: prevents concurrent access to other resources)\\n  4. Stop transaction coordinator\\n  5. Stop group/share coordinators\\n  6. Stop replica manager\\n  7. Shutdown alter partition manager\\n  8. Close log manager and remote storage\\n  9. Stop quota managers\\n  10. Final socket server shutdown\\n  11. Close lifecycle manager\\n\\n**BrokerLifecycleManager.scala** (lines 223-307): Manages broker state machine lifecycle\\n- **start()**: Sends StartupEvent through event queue\\n  - Initializes channel manager\\n  - Transitions to STARTING state\\n  - Sends broker registration\\n- **beginControlledShutdown()**: Graceful shutdown request\\n- **beginShutdown()**: Non-blocking shutdown initiation\\n- **close()**: Blocks until complete shutdown\\n- Event-driven architecture using KafkaEventQueue for thread-safe operations\\n\\n**KafkaRaftServer.scala**: Combined controller/broker with startup() and shutdown() methods\\n- Implements reverse-order shutdown: broker first, then controller\\n\\n#### 3. Test Framework (test-common/test-common-runtime/)\\n**KafkaClusterTestKit.java**: Demonstrates coordinated startup and shutdown of multiple broker/controller instances\\n- Uses ExecutorService for parallel startup\\n- Implements synchronized shutdown pattern\\n\\n#### 4. Mirror Maker (connect/mirror/)\\n**MirrorMaker.java** (lines 178-226): Multi-herder lifecycle coordinator\\n- **start()**: Creates CountDownLatches, starts all herders in try-finally blocks\\n- **stop()**: Stops all herders in try-finally blocks, ensuring each herder is stopped even if others fail\\n- **awaitStop()**: Waits for all herders to complete shutdown\\n\\n### Lifecycle Patterns Observed\\n\\n#### 1. Startup Ordering\\nServices are started in dependency order:\\n- Herder (manages everything)\\n  - Worker (manages connectors/tasks)\\n    - GlobalOffsetBackingStore\\n    - StatusBackingStore\\n    - ConfigBackingStore\\n  - REST server (depends on herder being ready)\\n\\n#### 2. Shutdown Ordering (Reverse Dependency Order)\\nServices are stopped in reverse order to their startup:\\n- REST server (stops accepting requests first)\\n- Herder (stops managing work)\\n  - Worker (stops connectors/tasks)\\n  - Backing stores\\n  - Status/Config stores\\n\\n#### 3. Error Handling Patterns\\n- **Try-finally blocks**: Ensure shutdown code runs even if errors occur\\n- **CoreUtils.swallow()**: Catches exceptions during shutdown to continue with remaining components\\n- **Utils.closeQuietly()**: Safe resource cleanup\\n- **CountDownLatches**: Coordinate multi-component startup/shutdown\\n- **AtomicBoolean flags**: Track shutdown state in thread-safe manner\\n\\n#### 4. Graceful Shutdown\\n- **Timeouts**: Workers use TASK_SHUTDOWN_GRACEFUL_TIMEOUT_MS (default 5000ms)\\n- **Hooks**: Register shutdown hooks that trigger stop() on JVM exit\\n- **Controlled shutdown**: Brokers support controlled shutdown mode before forceful termination\\n- **State transitions**: BrokerLifecycleManager uses state machine (RUNNING \u2192 PENDING_CONTROLLED_SHUTDOWN \u2192 SHUTTING_DOWN)\\n\\n#### 5. Thread Safety\\n- **Volatile variables**: For cross-thread visibility\\n- **Event queues**: Single-threaded processing with queued events\\n- **Executor services**: Shutdown gracefully with timeouts\\n- **Synchronized blocks**: Protect concurrent access\\n\\n### Key File References\\n\\n**Connect Lifecycle Chain**:\\n1. Connect.start() \u2192 herder.start() \u2192 (worker.start() \u2192 rest.initializeServer())\\n2. Connect.stop() \u2192 rest.stop() \u2192 herder.stop() \u2192 worker.stop()\\n\\n**Broker Lifecycle Chain**:\\n1. BrokerServer.startup() \u2192 BrokerLifecycleManager.start() \u2192 multiple component startups\\n2. BrokerServer.shutdown() \u2192 11-step reverse-order shutdown sequence\\n\\n**Mirror Maker Pattern**:\\n- Manages multiple Herder instances\\n- Ensures all herders start even if one fails\\n- Ensures all herders stop even if one fails\\n- Uses CountDownLatches for synchronization\\n\\nThis lifecycle pattern ensures that no service attempts to use resources that have already been shut down, and that all graceful shutdown procedures are completed before the JVM terminates.\"\n}\n",
